10.1016/j.jmp.2013.01.003
Although it appeared already in the work of Norbert Wiener under a different name (see e.g.  Fishburn and Monjardet (1992)  and  Wiener (1914, 1919–1920) ), the concept of a semiorder is usually attributed to R. Duncan Luce, who coined the term in 1956 (see  Luce (1956) ). The main aim of Luce was to develop a mathematical model of preferences able to capture situations of intransitive indifference with a threshold of discrimination or perception.

Let us recall that the original definition introduced by R.D. Luce in  Luce (1956)  is the following:

This notion was also studied in  Scott and Suppes (1958) , trying to identify a semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                       defined on a set  
                         S 
                       with a subset of the real line  
                         R 
                       endowed with the binary relation  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               R 
                            
                         
                       given by  
                         x 
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               R 
                            
                         
                         y 
                         
                         ⟺ 
                         
                         x 
                         + 
                         1 
                         < 
                         y 
                         
                         
                            ( 
                            x 
                            , 
                            y 
                            ∈ 
                            R 
                            ) 
                         
                      . The identification (if any) is made through a real-valued function (also known as “ utility ”)  
                         u 
                         : 
                         S 
                         → 
                         R 
                       such that  
                         a 
                         P 
                         b 
                         
                         ⟺ 
                         
                         u 
                         
                            ( 
                            a 
                            ) 
                         
                         
                            
                               P

                            b 
                            )

                            a

                            )

                            S

                      . Here the constant 1 acts as a “ threshold of utility discrimination or perception ”.

Whenever this type of representation does exist, then the pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            u 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ) 
                         
                       is usually called in the literature a  Scott–Suppes representation  of  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                       (see  Scott and Suppes (1958) ).

This kind of numerical representation, where in particular  the threshold of perception is a positive constant , is encountered in a wide range of applications, as extensive measurement in Mathematical Psychology (see  Krantz (1967)  and  Lehrer and Wagner (1985) ), choice theory under risk (see  Fishburn (1968) ), decision-making under risk (see  Rubinstein (1988) ), or modellization of choice with errors (see  Agaev and Aleskerov (1993) ).

At this stage, it is important to point out that, with the formal definition of a semiorder introduced by R.D. Luce, not all the semiorders are representable (see  Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2002)  and  Induráin (2008) ). In addition, nowadays it is usual to deal with alternative definitions of the notion of a semiorder, being  equivalent  to the original one introduced by Luce in ( Luce, 1956 ), but perhaps more intuitive or suitable to be handled. For instance, we may deal with the concept of a semiorder understood as an asymmetric Ferrers and semitransitive binary relation defined on a nonempty set.

Thus, a semiorder can also be interpreted as a particular case of an interval order, in which the threshold of discrimination is no longer constant. What makes interval orders and semiorders so interesting is the fact that  the strict preference relation is transitive, whereas the indifference relation may be intransitive  (see e.g.  Bridges (1983) ,  Fishburn (1970a,b)  and  Tversky (1969) ). Both concepts, namely semiorders and interval orders, can be formalized in terms of different equivalent compositions of preference and indifference relations (see  Proposition 2.7 ,  Lemma 2.9  and  Theorem 2.10  below).

Classical studies on semiorders appeared in  Beja and Gilboa (1992) ,  Fishburn (1970b) ,  Fishburn (1973) ,  Fishburn (1985) ,  Gensemer (1987) ,  Krantz (1967) ,  Lehrer and Wagner (1985) ,  Manders (1981) ,  Monjardet (1978) ,  Nakamura (2002) ,  Pirlot and Vincke (1997) ,  Rabinovitch (1977) ,  Scott and Suppes (1958)  and  S̀wistak (1980) , or more recently in Section 6.6 of  Aleskerov, Bouyssou, and Monjardet (2007) .

It was proved in  Scott and Suppes (1958)  (see also  Rabinovitch (1977) ) that every semiorder defined on a  finite  set  
                         S 
                       actually admits a Scott–Suppes representation. The situations in which  
                         S 
                       is  infinite  have also been analyzed in the specialized literature. For the particular case when  
                         S 
                       is  countable , a characterization of the Scott–Suppes representability was obtained in  Manders (1981)  as well as in  Beja and Gilboa (1992) .

Several authors commented upon the difficulties that the analysis of the  uncountable  case carries (see, e.g., Section 4 in  Beja and Gilboa (1992) ), sometimes avoiding the consideration of a Scott–Suppes representation (that could actually be quite restrictive, as stated on p. 126 in  S̀wistak (1980) ), and substituting it by milder forms of representations (e.g., through different kinds of intervals) that could be more easily characterized. Nevertheless, the existence of a Scott–Suppes representation immediately implies the existence of many other kinds of representations analyzed in the literature (e.g., by means of intervals in the sense of  Fishburn (1973) , GNR representations in the sense of  Beja and Gilboa (1992) , etc.).

More recently, several complete characterizations of the existence of Scott–Suppes representations of semiorders, valid for the general case, were finally achieved in  Candeal et al. (2002) . But all those characterizations introduced in  Candeal et al. (2002)  lean on the consideration of some  extension  of a totally preordered structure that is closely related to the given semiorder. 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         At an intuitive level, what we mean here is that the given semiorder has closely related a total preorder such that, in order for the semiorder to have a Scott–Suppes representation, its corresponding related total preorder must have an extension conforming to certain properties (see e.g.  Abrísqueta, Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2009)  and  Candeal et al. (2002)  for further details). 
                       Unfortunately, the suitable extensions could be difficult to visualize, and not determined  a priori  in an easy standard manner.

Due to this fact, the problem of finding an  intrinsic  characterization of the Scott–Suppes representability of a semiorder was considered in several papers. Partial results were obtained in  Abrísqueta et al. (2009) , and, using different techniques, the main general problem was finally solved in  Candeal and Induráin (2010) . (Here, by “intrinsic” we mean characterizations that only depend on the given set and semiorder, that is,  no extension is involved ).

A semiorder being a special case of an interval order, it seems relevant to revise some key facts concerning the numerical representability of interval orders. In particular, we may study different characterizations of the representability of an interval order by means of two real-valued functions, in the search for new characterizations of the Scott–Suppes representability of semiorders. These ideas were already introduced in  Abrísqueta et al. (2009) , where the following conjecture appears (see  Abrísqueta et al. (2009, Conjecture 6.3, pp. 260–261) ): “ If a semiorder defined on a nonempty set  
                      
                         S 
                       
                      is regular with respect to sequences, and representable as an interval order, then it is also representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes ”. (In the present paper, the key notion of  regularity  will be introduced below in Section  4 . See  Definition 4.2 ).

In Section  4  of the present paper, we will prove that the conjecture is  true . As a matter of fact, we will show that  in the presence of regularity, any characterization of the numerical representability of an interval order by means of two real-valued functions  (see e.g.  Bosi, Campión, Candeal, and Induráin (2007) ,  Bosi, Candeal, Induráin, Olóriz, and Zudaire (2001) ,  Bosi, Gutiérrez García, and Induráin (2009) ,  Bridges and Mehta (1995) ,  Campión, Candeal, and Induráin (2006) ,  Doignon, Ducamp, and Falmagne (1984) ,  Fishburn (1973) ,  Fishburn (1985)  and  Olóriz, Candeal, and Induráin (1998) )  immediately induces a characterization of the Scott–Suppes representability of a semiorder .

Therefore, the difficult problem of finding a characterization of the Scott–Suppes representability of a semiorder (in the general case) not only has one possible solution, as the characterization obtained in  Candeal and Induráin (2010) , but as many ones as characterizations of the numerical representability of interval orders. (For instance, Theorem 1 in  Bosi et al. (2001)  already furnishes ten characterizations).

The structure of the paper goes as follows .

In Section  2  we introduce basic definitions and background results. In Section  3  we study different characterizations of the numerical representability of interval orders, paying special attention to  separability  properties. In Section  4  we show that the necessary and sufficient conditions for the representability of interval orders induce characterizations of the Scott–Suppes representability of a semiorder, provided that the key condition of regularity with respect to sequences is satisfied. Section  5  with further comments and a final Section  6  of conclusions close the paper.

a  total preorder  if it is transitive and total,

a  partial order  if it is reflexive, antisymmetric and transitive,

a  total order  (also known as a  linear order ) if it is antisymmetric, transitive and total,

a  weak order  if it is asymmetric and negatively transitive,

an  interval order  if it is irreflexive and Ferrers.

Remark 2.3 
                            It follows from the previous  Definition 2.2  that an irreflexive Ferrers relation (i.e., an interval order) is also asymmetric and transitive. 
                          In the following definition, the symbol  
                            ∘ 
                          stands for the usual relational composition. Although it has already been stated in Section  1 , we recall again the original definition of a semiorder, due to R. D. Luce (see  Luce (1956) ).

Definition 2.4 
                            
                               Luce, 1956 
                            
                            A  semiorder  on  
                                  X 
                                is a couple  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     P 
                                     , 
                                     I 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                of relations 
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                                  As commented by an anonymous referee, the reader could be a bit surprised by the fact that throughout the paper we present a semiorder as a pair  
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           P 
                                           , 
                                           I 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                      of binary relations, while interval orders, preorders, weak orders, etc., are presented as single relations. One may wonder if there is any advantage in our presentation, and perhaps the answer is negative. However, we wanted to keep here the original definition (see  Luce (1956) ), maybe for historical reasons and to pay homage to R. D. Luce. At this stage, we point out that as early as in the paper by Scott and Suppes (see  Scott and Suppes (1958) ), a semiorder is already introduced as a single relation (usually  
                                        P 
                                     ). Of course, our option of presentation is indeed equivalent to the definition as a single relation (see  Theorem 2.10  below). Although with our “historical” option a semiorder would not be, formally, an interval order, since it has been defined using two binary functions instead of just one,  Theorem 2.10  comes to the rescue and puts things in order. Thus, by the equivalence (a)⇔(b) in  Theorem 2.10 , one may directly define a semiorder as the corresponding relation  
                                        P 
                                      of the pair  
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           P 
                                           , 
                                           I 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                      in Luce’ s original definition. Therefore, this  
                                        P 
                                      is asymmetric, Ferrers and semitransitive, and a semiorder becomes an interval order of a particular kind.

Remark 2.8 
                            Due to the fact expressed by the characterization given in  Proposition 2.7 , some authors use the notion of a semiorder for the couple  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     P 
                                     , 
                                     I 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               , but other authors use the term “semiorder” to refer, directly, to an irreflexive relation  
                                  P 
                               , defined on a set  
                                  X 
                               , such that  
                                  P 
                                is both Ferrers and semitransitive, or, equivalently, such that  
                                  P 
                                is a semitransitive interval order.

Definition 3.3 
                         Let  
                               X 
                             be a nonempty set. 
                         A total preorder

                             defined on  
                               X

                               u 
                               : 
                               X 
                               → 
                               R

                               x 
                               Q

                               x

                               y 
                               ∈ 
                               X

                               X

                               = 
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     a 
                                  
                               
                             is a representable total preorder. Equivalently, if there exists a real-valued function (called a  utility function , too)

Proof 
                         The result stated in  Proposition 3.5  is well-known in this literature. See e.g.  Candeal and Induráin (1990a,b) , the first three chapters of  Bridges and Mehta (1995) , or  Olóriz et al. (1998) .  □

The result stated in  Proposition 3.5  is well-known in this literature. See e.g.  Candeal and Induráin (1990a,b) , the first three chapters of  Bridges and Mehta (1995) , or  Olóriz et al. (1998) .  □

By means of these separability conditions, the representability is characterized as follows.

Theorem 3.6 
                         
                            Let  
                            
                               X 
                             
                            be a nonempty set. 
                            
                               
                                  (a) 
                                  
                                     A total preorder  
                                     
                                        Q 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is perfectly separable. 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (b) 
                                  
                                     An interval order  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is i.o.-separable.

Let  
                            
                               X 
                             
                            be a nonempty set. 
                            
                               
                                  (a) 
                                  
                                     A total preorder  
                                     
                                        Q 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is perfectly separable. 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (b) 
                                  
                                     An interval order  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is i.o.-separable.

A total preorder  
                                     
                                        Q 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is perfectly separable.

An interval order  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     defined on  
                                     
                                        X 
                                      
                                     is representable if and only if it is i.o.-separable.

Proof 
                         Both results are well-known. For instance, part (a) may be seen, as well as similar results, in  Bridges and Mehta (1995) . In addition, part (b) is proved in  Olóriz et al. (1998)  or  Bosi et al. (2001) .  □

Both results are well-known. For instance, part (a) may be seen, as well as similar results, in  Bridges and Mehta (1995) . In addition, part (b) is proved in  Olóriz et al. (1998)  or  Bosi et al. (2001) .  □

Several other characterizations of the representability of an interval order have been achieved in the specialized literature. In particular, in Theorems 1 and 3 in  Bosi et al. (2001)  the following facts were stated (see  Theorem 3.7  below). As commented in Section 3 of  Bosi et al. (2001) , the techniques used to prove that result come from a large variety of frameworks, as functional equations (see e.g.  Olóriz et al. (1998) ) or measure theory (see e.g.  Bosi and Isler (1995)  and  Candeal-Haro and Induráin-Eraso (1993) ) among others.

Theorems 3.6 and 3.7  above furnish characterizations of the representability of total preorders and interval orders in terms of certain  separability  conditions.

In this  Section  4   we are interested in characterizing the Scott–Suppes representability of semiorders in terms of some separability condition .

As an immediate consequence of  Theorem 3.6  we may observe that every total preorder (respectively, every interval order) defined on a  countable  set  
                         X 
                       is representable. The obvious reason is that when  
                         X 
                       is countable it happens that every total preorder on  
                         X 
                       is perfectly separable, and, in the same way, every interval order is i.o.-separable.

A similar result is no longer true for semiorders, as  Example 4.1  shows next.

Remark 4.4 
                         We immediately observe that the above condition of regularity is a necessary condition for a semiorder  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , to be representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes.

We immediately observe that the above condition of regularity is a necessary condition for a semiorder  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , to be representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes.

As a matter of fact, in the case of a semiorder defined on a  countable  set  
                         X 
                      , regularity is not only necessary, but also sufficient to guarantee the Scott–Suppes representability, as  Theorem 4.5  states next.

Theorem 4.5 
                         
                            Let  
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I 
                                  ) 
                               
                             
                            be a semiorder defined on a countable set  
                            
                               X 
                             
                            . Then the semiorder  
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I 
                                  ) 
                               
                             
                            is representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes if and only if it is regular.

Let  
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I

                                  ( 
                                  P 
                                  , 
                                  I 
                                  ) 
                               
                             
                            is representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes if and only if it is regular.

Proof 
                         See Proposition 8 on pp. 237–239 in  Manders (1981) . (Similar results also appear in Theorems 3.7 and 3.8 on pp. 435–448 in  Beja & Gilboa, 1992 .)  □

See Proposition 8 on pp. 237–239 in  Manders (1981) . (Similar results also appear in Theorems 3.7 and 3.8 on pp. 435–448 in  Beja & Gilboa, 1992 .)  □

Once we know that the regularity with respect to sequences is a necessary condition for the Scott–Suppes representability of a semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , defined on a nonempty set  
                         X 
                      , we may search for  separability  conditions for semiorders (in the spirit of the ones introduced for total preorders and interval orders, namely: perfect separability and i.o.-separability). Thus, we may look for characterizations of the Scott–Suppes representability of semiorders stated in terms of a suitable separability condition plus the necessary regularity with respect to sequences. This long-standing problem was finally solved in  Candeal and Induráin (2010)  (see  Theorem 4.7  below).

The separability condition introduced to this achievement reads as follows.

The main result in  Candeal and Induráin (2010)  proves the following.

Theorem 4.7

                               s 
                            
                            -separable.

                               X

                                  P 
                                  ,

A glance at the proof of  Theorem 4.7  given in  Candeal and Induráin (2010)  shows that the techniques used to achieve that result are quite different from the ones arising in characterizations of the representability of interval orders by means of two real-valued functions, as in Theorem 1 in  Bosi et al. (2001)  (see  Theorem 3.7  above). We would like now to retrieve the ideas involved in such a result, adapting them to the case of semiorders and Scott–Suppes representability. Along this line, in  Abrísqueta et al. (2009)  it was conjectured that if a semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , defined on a nonempty set  
                         X 
                      , is regular and its strict preference  
                         P 
                       is representable  as an interval order  through a pair of real-valued functions, then the semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , is representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes (see Conjecture 6.3 in  Abrísqueta et al. (2009, pp 260–261) ). Observe that this would imply, in particular, that whenever  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , is a semiorder that satisfies the condition of regularity with respect to sequences, then its strict preference  
                         P 
                       is i.o.-separable if and only if the semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                       is  
                         s 
                      -separable.  Now we are ready to prove the conjecture  (see  Theorem 4.11  below in Section  4 ).

As a matter of fact, we prove more, namely  any condition equivalent to the i.o.-separability of  
                      
                         P 
                       
                      as an interval order is equivalent to the  
                      
                         s 
                      - separability of  
                      
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , without making use of the regularity condition.

To do so, first we need to introduce a definition and a lemma.

Thus, as mentioned before in  Theorem 3.6 , the i.o.-separability condition characterizes the representability of an interval order  
                         P 
                       defined on a nonempty set  
                         X 
                      . However, there are other properties closely related to i.o.-separability, that we collect by means of  Definition 4.8 .

Remark 4.9 
                         Remember that given an interval order  
                               P 
                            , defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , the associated relation  
                               R 
                             is a total preorder if and only if  
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            , and  
                               R 
                             coincide. Also, if  
                               Q 
                             is a total preorder on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , its adjoint  
                               
                                  
                                     Q 
                                  
                                  
                                     a 
                                  
                               
                            , that is a weak order, can also be considered, in particular, as an interval order on  
                               X 
                            . 
                         In addition, it is straightforward to see that the following conditions are equivalent for a total preorder  
                               Q 
                             defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                             (compare with  Proposition 3.5 ): 
                         
                            
                               
                                  (a) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is Cantor-separable, 
                               
                               
                                  (b) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is strongly separable, 
                               
                               
                                  (c) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is perfectly separable and full.

Remember that given an interval order  
                               P 
                            , defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , the associated relation  
                               R 
                             is a total preorder if and only if  
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                               , 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            , and  
                               R 
                             coincide. Also, if  
                               Q 
                             is a total preorder on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                            , its adjoint  
                               
                                  
                                     Q 
                                  
                                  
                                     a 
                                  
                               
                            , that is a weak order, can also be considered, in particular, as an interval order on  
                               X 
                            .

In addition, it is straightforward to see that the following conditions are equivalent for a total preorder  
                               Q 
                             defined on a nonempty set  
                               X 
                             (compare with  Proposition 3.5 ):

(a) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is Cantor-separable, 
                               
                               
                                  (b) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is strongly separable, 
                               
                               
                                  (c) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is perfectly separable and full.

Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is Cantor-separable,

Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is strongly separable,

Q 
                                           
                                           
                                              a 
                                           
                                        
                                      is perfectly separable and full.

Let us recall the hierarchy among the properties just introduced in  Definition 4.8 .

Lemma 4.10 
                         
                            Let  
                            
                               X 
                             
                            be a nonempty set. Let  
                            
                               P 
                             
                            be an interval order defined on  
                            
                               X 
                             
                            . The following properties hold: 
                            
                               
                                  (a) 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is Cantor-separable  
                                     
                                        ⟹ 
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is strongly separable  
                                     
                                        ⟹ 
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is i.o.-separable  
                                     
                                        ⟹ 
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is perfectly separable  
                                     
                                        ⟹ 
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is weakly separable. The converse implications are not true, in general. 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (b) 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is strongly separable  
                                     
                                        
                                        ⟺ 
                                        
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is i.o.-separable and full. 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (c) 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is strongly separable if and only if there exists a countable subset  
                                     
                                        D 
                                        ⊆ 
                                        X 
                                      
                                     such that for every  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        , 
                                        y 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                      
                                     with  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        P 
                                        y 
                                     
                                     there exists  
                                     
                                        d 
                                        ∈ 
                                        D 
                                      
                                     such that  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        
                                           
                                              P 
                                           
                                           
                                              ∗ 
                                           
                                        
                                        d 
                                        P 
                                        y 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (d) 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is strongly separable if and only if there exists a countable subset  
                                     
                                        D 
                                        ⊆ 
                                        X 
                                      
                                     such that for every  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        , 
                                        y 
                                        ∈ 
                                        X 
                                      
                                     with  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        P 
                                        y 
                                     
                                     there exists  
                                     
                                        d 
                                        ∈ 
                                        D 
                                      
                                     such that  
                                     
                                        x 
                                        P 
                                        d 
                                        
                                           
                                              P 
                                           
                                           
                                              ∗ 
                                              ∗ 
                                           
                                        
                                        y 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  (e) 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     is i.o.-separable  
                                     
                                        
                                        ⟺ 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                              R 
                                           
                                           
                                              ∗ 
                                           
                                        
                                      
                                     is a perfectly separable total preorder, and  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     has at most a countable number of non-left-similar holes  
                                     
                                        
                                        ⟺ 
                                        
                                        
                                           
                                              R 
                                           
                                           
                                              ∗ 
                                              ∗ 
                                           
                                        
                                      
                                     is a perfectly separable total preorder, and  
                                     
                                        P 
                                      
                                     has at most a countable number of non-right-similar holes.

A classical question in the study of the numerical representability of certain kinds of orderings (e.g. total preorders, interval orders, semiorders) is that of the existence of a “ germ of non-representability ”. For a germ we understand a minimal substructure that immediately provokes the non-representability of the given structure.

For the case of interval orders, the idea of studying possible minimal germs of non-representability appears implicitly in  Mitas (1995)  by means of the concept of a “ forbidden suborder ”. For several other ordered structures and different kinds of representations this problem has been completely solved (see e.g.  Candeal, De Miguel, and Induráin (1998)  for the case of commutative totally ordered semigroups using representations by means of order-preserving real-valued semigroup monomorphisms. See also  Beardon, Candeal, Herden, Induráin, and Mehta (2002)  for the case of the representation of totally ordered sets through a real-valued order-preserving function).

In the particular case of semiorders, for a  germ of non-representability in the sense of Scott and Suppes  we mean a semiordered structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            Z 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  I 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                      , where  
                         Z 
                       is a nonempty set and  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  I 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       is a semiorder defined on  
                         Z 
                      , such that if a nonempty set  
                         X 
                       is endowed with a semiorder  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                       that is not representable in the sense of Scott and Suppes, then the semiordered structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            X 
                            , 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , contains up-to-isomorphism, a copy of  
                         
                            ( 
                            Z 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  P 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  I 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       or a copy of its dual structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            Z 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     
                                        
                                           P 
                                        
                                        
                                           Z 
                                        
                                     
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  t 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  I 
                               
                               
                                  Z 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       where  
                         a 
                         
                            
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        P 
                                     
                                     
                                        Z 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                            
                               t 
                            
                         
                         b 
                         
                         ⟺ 
                         
                         b 
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               Z 
                            
                         
                         a 
                         
                         
                            ( 
                            a 
                            , 
                            b 
                            ∈ 
                            Z 
                            ) 
                         
                      .

By  Theorem 4.5  the structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            Y 
                            , 
                            < 
                            , 
                            = 
                            ) 
                         
                      , where  
                         Y 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            0 
                            } 
                         
                         ∪ 
                         
                            { 
                            
                               
                                  2 
                               
                               
                                  − 
                                  n 
                               
                            
                            : 
                            n 
                            ∈ 
                            N 
                            } 
                         
                      , and “ 
                         < 
                      ” (respectively, “ 
                         = 
                      ”) denotes the usual order (respectively, the equality) on the real line  
                         R 
                      , is a germ of non-representability for semiorders  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                       defined on a  countable  nonempty set  
                         X 
                      . In addition,  Theorem 4.11  tells us more: indeed the structure  
                         
                            ( 
                            Y 
                            , 
                            < 
                            , 
                            = 
                            ) 
                         
                      , is a germ of non-representability in the sense of Scott and Suppes for semiorders  
                         
                            ( 
                            P 
                            , 
                            I 
                            ) 
                         
                      , defined on a nonempty set  
                         X 
                       (countable or not), whose corresponding strict preference  
                         P 
                       is an i.o.-separable interval order.

It is important to point out that the conditions of regularity with respect to sequences, and that of semiorder separability ( 
                         s 
                      -separability) are  independent , in general, as proved in Theorem 3.7 in  Candeal and Induráin (2010) .

In what concerns the quest of a characterization of the semiorders that possess a Scott–Suppes representation, i.e., a representation by a real valued function and a constant threshold, this paper could perhaps be seen as the “final chapter”.

Nevertheless, about the theory of representability of semiorders, some unanswered questions remain. An important open problem at this stage consists in characterizing the  continuous  representability of semiorders defined on topological spaces. As far as we know, only partial results that cover some relevant special cases have been issued (see e.g.  Bosi (1995) ,  Bosi, Campión et al. (2007) ,  Bosi, Candeal, and Induráin (2007) ,  Campión, Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2008) ,  Candeal, Induráin, and Zudaire (2004) ,  Chateauneuf (1987)  and  Gensemer (1987) ).

Another trend for future research on semiorders, as well as on other different kinds of ordered structures, deals with  graded  preferences and consequently uses  fuzzy  set theory. Thus, when we define a crisp (or non-fuzzy) binary relation  
                         Q 
                       on a nonempty set  
                         X 
                       we are considering a subset, that we denote again by  
                         Q 
                      , of the Cartesian product  
                         X 
                         × 
                         X 
                      , so that a pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            x 
                            , 
                            y 
                            ) 
                         
                       belongs to  
                         Q 
                       whenever the element  
                         x 
                       is related to the element  
                         y 
                       through  
                         Q 
                      , which is also written as  
                         x 
                         Q 
                         y 
                      . However, when using  graded  preferences, the key concept is that of a  fuzzy binary relation  
                      
                         Q 
                      , now framed as a fuzzy subset of the Cartesian product  
                         X 
                         × 
                         X 
                      . In this case,  
                         Q 
                       is a map defined on  
                         X 
                         × 
                         X 
                       and taking values on  
                         
                            [ 
                            0 
                            , 
                            1 
                            ] 
                         
                      , so that given two elements  
                         x 
                         , 
                         y 
                         ∈ 
                         X 
                       the number  
                         Q 
                         
                            ( 
                            x 
                            , 
                            y 
                            ) 
                         
                       expresses the grade of relationship (valued between “0” or no relationship at all and “1” or full relationship) existing between the element  
                         x 
                       and the element  
                         y 
                      . In this new context, the “crisp” definitions of the classical concepts of a total preorder, an interval order and a semiorder, as well as the notion of representability, should be extended to the fuzzy setting. It may happen that several equivalences arising in the crisp approach (e.g., those in  Theorem 2.10  for the concept of a semiorder) are no longer valid in the fuzzy approach, so giving rise to a new hierarchy of non-equivalent possible definitions. As far as we know, this theory of graded preferences and numerical representability furnishes an interesting field of research activity, that has recently been launched to the specialized literature (see e.g.  Agud, Catalán, Díaz, and Induráin (2012) ,  Bodenhofer, De Baets, and Fodor (2007) ,  Díaz, De Baets et al. (2011) ,  Díaz, De Baets, and Montes (2012) ,  Díaz, Induráin et al. (2011) ,  Induráin, Martinetti, Montes, Díaz, and Abrísqueta (2011) ,  Montes, Díaz, and Montes (2010)  and  Ovchivnnikov (2000) ).

Last but not least, another stream of on-going research on the representability of ordered structures tries to discover some suitable codomain, perhaps different from the real line, in order to represent there the classical structures (total preorders, interval orders and semiorders) using  only a single function , instead of the two functions needed for the classical representability of interval orders, or the function plus a threshold that correspond to the Scott–Suppes representability of semiorders (see e.g.  Bosi et al. (2009) ,  Candeal, Gutiérrez García, and Induráin (2009)  and  Estevan, Gutiérrez García, and Induráin (in press) ).
